<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thailand_Product_Exports_(2019).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="715" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Thailand_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg/220px-Thailand_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Thailand_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg/330px-Thailand_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Thailand_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg/440px-Thailand_Product_Exports_%282019%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thailand_Product_Exports_(2019).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A proportional representation of Thailand exports, 2019</div></div></div>
<p>The economy of Thailand is heavily export-dependent, with exports accounting for more than two-thirds of gross domestic product (GDP). Thailand exports over US$105 billion worth of goods and services annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup> Major exports include cars, computers, electrical appliances, <a href="/wiki/Rice_production_in_Thailand" title="Rice production in Thailand">rice</a>, textiles and footwear, fishery products, rubber, and jewellery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thailand is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emerging_markets" title="Emerging markets">emerging economy</a> and is considered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newly_industrialised_country" title="Newly industrialised country">newly industrialised country</a>. Thailand had a 2017 GDP of US$1.236 trillion (on a <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> basis).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[126]</a></sup> Thailand is the second largest economy in Southeast Asia after Indonesia. Thailand ranks midway in the wealth spread in Southeast Asia as it is the fourth richest nation according to GDP per capita, after Singapore, Brunei, and Malaysia.
</p><p>Thailand functions as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anchor_economy" title="Anchor economy">anchor economy</a> for the neighbouring developing economies of Laos, Myanmar, and Cambodia. In the third quarter of 2014, the unemployment rate in Thailand stood at 0.84% according to Thailand's National Economic and Social Development Board (NESDB).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_economic_history">Recent economic history</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bangkok_(251385359).jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1305" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Bangkok_%28251385359%29.jpeg/220px-Bangkok_%28251385359%29.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Bangkok_%28251385359%29.jpeg/330px-Bangkok_%28251385359%29.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Bangkok_%28251385359%29.jpeg/440px-Bangkok_%28251385359%29.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bangkok_(251385359).jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sathorn" title="Sathorn">Sathorn</a> in Bangkok is a skyscraper-studded business district that is home to major hotels and embassies.</div></div></div>
<p>Thailand experienced the world's highest economic growth rate from 1985 to 1996 – averaging 12.4% annually. In 1997 increased pressure on the <a href="/wiki/Thai_baht" title="Thai baht">baht</a>, a year in which the economy contracted by 1.9%, led to a crisis that uncovered financial sector weaknesses and forced the <a href="/wiki/Chavalit_Yongchaiyudh" title="Chavalit Yongchaiyudh">Chavalit Yongchaiyudh</a> administration to <a href="/wiki/Floating_exchange_rate" title="Floating exchange rate">float the currency</a>. Prime Minister Chavalit Yongchaiyudh was forced to resign after his cabinet came under fire for its slow response to the economic crisis. The baht was pegged at 25 to the US dollar from 1978 to 1997. The baht reached its lowest point of 56 to the US dollar in January 1998 and the economy contracted by 10.8% that year, triggering the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_financial_crisis" title="Asian financial crisis">Asian financial crisis</a>.
</p><p>Thailand's economy started to recover in 1999, expanding 4.2–4.4% in 2000, thanks largely to strong exports. Growth (2.2%) was dampened by the softening of the global economy in 2001, but picked up in the subsequent years owing to strong growth in Asia, a relatively weak baht encouraging exports, and increased domestic spending as a result of several mega projects and incentives of Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Thaksin_Shinawatra" title="Thaksin Shinawatra">Thaksin Shinawatra</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Thaksinomics" title="Thaksinomics">Thaksinomics</a>. Growth in 2002, 2003, and 2004 was 5–7% annually.
</p><p>Growth in 2005, 2006, and 2007 hovered around 4–5%. Due both to the weakening of the US dollar and an increasingly strong Thai currency, by March 2008 the dollar was hovering around the 33 baht mark. While Thaksinomics has received criticism, official economic data reveals that between 2001 and 2011, Isan's GDP per capita more than doubled to US$1,475, while, over the same period, GDP in the Bangkok area increased from US$7,900 to nearly US$13,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the instability surrounding major 2010 protests, the GDP growth of Thailand settled at around 4–5%, from highs of 5–7% under the previous civilian administration. Political uncertainty was identified as the primary cause of a decline in investor and consumer confidence. The IMF predicted that the Thai economy would rebound strongly from the low 0.1% GDP growth in 2011, to 5.5% in 2012 and then 7.5% in 2013, due to the monetary policy of the Bank of Thailand, as well as a package of fiscal stimulus measures introduced by the former <a href="/wiki/Yingluck_Shinawatra" title="Yingluck Shinawatra">Yingluck Shinawatra</a> government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the Thai military coup of 22 May 2014. In 2017, Concluded with information on the Thai economy's grew an inflation-adjusted 3.9%, up from 3.3% in 2016, marking its fastest expansion since 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Income.2C_poverty_and_wealth"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Income,_poverty_and_wealth">Income, poverty and wealth</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GDP_per_capita_development_in_Thailand.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="652" data-file-width="1028" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/GDP_per_capita_development_in_Thailand.jpg/220px-GDP_per_capita_development_in_Thailand.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/GDP_per_capita_development_in_Thailand.jpg/330px-GDP_per_capita_development_in_Thailand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/GDP_per_capita_development_in_Thailand.jpg/440px-GDP_per_capita_development_in_Thailand.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GDP_per_capita_development_in_Thailand.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Development of real GDP per capita, 1890 to 2018</div></div></div>
<p>Thais have median wealth per one adult person of $1,469 in 2016,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wealth16_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-wealth16-142">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 98">: 98 </span></sup> increasing from $605 in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wealth16_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-wealth16-142">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 34">: 34 </span></sup> In 2016, Thailand was <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Human_Development_Index" title="List of countries by Human Development Index">ranked 87th in Human Development Index</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_inequality-adjusted_HDI" title="List of countries by inequality-adjusted HDI">70th in the inequality-adjusted HDI</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, Thailand's median household income was ฿26,946 per month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nso60_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-nso60-144">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1">: 1 </span></sup> Top quintile households had a 45.0% share of all income, while bottom quintile households had 7.1%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nso60_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-nso60-144">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 4">: 4 </span></sup> There were 26.9 million persons who had the bottom 40% of income earning less than ฿5,344 per person per month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-poverty59_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-poverty59-145">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 5">: 5 </span></sup> During <a href="/wiki/2013%E2%80%932014_Thai_political_crisis" title="2013–2014 Thai political crisis">2013–2014 Thai political crisis</a>, a survey found that anti-government <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Democratic_Reform_Committee" title="People's Democratic Reform Committee">PDRC</a> mostly (32%) had a monthly income of more than ฿50,000, while pro-government <a href="/wiki/United_Front_for_Democracy_Against_Dictatorship" title="United Front for Democracy Against Dictatorship">UDD</a> mostly (27%) had between ฿10,000 and ฿20,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7">: 7 </span></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, <a href="/wiki/Credit_Suisse" title="Credit Suisse">Credit Suisse</a> reported that Thailand was the world's third most unequal country, behind Russia and India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcworth17_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbcworth17-147">[136]</a></sup> The top 10% richest held 79% of the country's assets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcworth17_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbcworth17-147">[136]</a></sup> The top 1% richest held 58% of the assets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcworth17_147-2"><a href="#cite_note-bbcworth17-147">[136]</a></sup> Thai 50 richest families had a total net worth accounting to 30% of GDP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcworth17_147-3"><a href="#cite_note-bbcworth17-147">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, 5.81 million people lived in poverty, or 11.6 million people (17.2% of population) if "near poor" is included.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-poverty59_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-poverty59-145">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1">: 1 </span></sup> Proportion of the poor relative to total population in each region was 12.96% in the Northeast, 12.35% in the South, and 9.83% in the North.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-poverty59_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-poverty59-145">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2">: 2 </span></sup> In 2017, there were 14 million people who applied for social welfare (yearly income of less than ฿100,000 was required).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcworth17_147-4"><a href="#cite_note-bbcworth17-147">[136]</a></sup> At the end of 2017, Thailand's total household debt was ฿11.76 trillion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-สังคม_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-สังคม-134">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 5">: 5 </span></sup> In 2010, 3% of all household were bankrupt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hhbot_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-hhbot-136">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 5">: 5 </span></sup> In 2016, there were estimated 30,000 homeless persons in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exports_and_manufacturing">Exports and manufacturing</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thailand_Export_Treemap.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="751" data-file-width="962" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thailand_Export_Treemap.png/250px-Thailand_Export_Treemap.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thailand_Export_Treemap.png/375px-Thailand_Export_Treemap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thailand_Export_Treemap.png/500px-Thailand_Export_Treemap.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thailand_Export_Treemap.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A proportional representation of Thailand's exports</div></div></div>
<p>The economy of Thailand is heavily export-dependent, with exports accounting for more than two-thirds of gross domestic product (GDP). Thailand exports over US$105 billion worth of goods and services annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup> Major exports include cars, computers, electrical appliances, <a href="/wiki/Rice_production_in_Thailand" title="Rice production in Thailand">rice</a>, textiles and footwear, fishery products, rubber, and jewellery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Substantial industries include electric appliances, components, computer components, and vehicles. Thailand's recovery from the 1997–1998 Asian financial crisis depended mainly on exports, among various other factors. As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Thailand&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry_in_Thailand" title="Automotive industry in Thailand">Thai automotive industry</a> was the largest in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_motor_vehicle_production" title="List of countries by motor vehicle production">9th largest in the world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BkP_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-BkP-149">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OICA_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-OICA-151">[140]</a></sup> The Thailand industry has an annual output of near 1.5 million vehicles, mostly commercial vehicles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OICA_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-OICA-151">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the vehicles built in Thailand are developed and licensed by foreign producers, mainly Japanese and American. The Thai car industry takes advantage of the <a href="/wiki/ASEAN_Free_Trade_Area" title="ASEAN Free Trade Area">ASEAN Free Trade Area</a> (AFTA) to find a market for many of its products. Eight manufacturers, five Japanese, two US, and <a href="/wiki/Tata_Group" title="Tata Group">Tata</a> of India, produce pick-up trucks in Thailand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nikkei_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nikkei-152">[141]</a></sup> As of 2012, Due to its favorable taxation for 2-door pick-ups at only 3–12% against 17–50% for passenger cars, Thailand was the second largest consumer of pick-up trucks in the world, after the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wards_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wards-153">[142]</a></sup> In 2014, pick-ups accounted for 42% of all new vehicle sales in Thailand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nikkei_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nikkei-152">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Thailand" title="Tourism in Thailand">Tourism in Thailand</a></div>
<p>Tourism makes up about 6% of the country's economy. Thailand was the most visited country in Southeast Asia in 2013, according to the World Tourism Organisation. Estimates of tourism receipts directly contributing to the Thai GDP of 12 trillion baht range from 9 percent (1 trillion baht) (2013) to 16 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[143]</a></sup> When including the indirect effects of tourism, it is said to account for 20.2 percent (2.4 trillion baht) of Thailand's GDP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WTTC-2014_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-WTTC-2014-155">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1">: 1 </span></sup>
</p><p>Asian tourists primarily visit Thailand for Bangkok and the historical, natural, and cultural sights in its vicinity. Western tourists not only visit Bangkok and surroundings, but in addition many travel to the southern beaches and islands. The north is the chief destination for trekking and <a href="/wiki/Adventure_travel" title="Adventure travel">adventure travel</a> with its diverse ethnic minority groups and forested mountains. The region hosting the fewest tourists is <a href="/wiki/Isan" title="Isan">Isan</a>. To accommodate foreign visitors, a separate tourism police with offices were set up in the major tourist areas and an emergency telephone number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thailand ranks fifth biggest <a href="/wiki/Medical_tourism" title="Medical tourism">medical tourism</a> destination of inbound medical tourism spending, according to <a href="/wiki/World_Travel_and_Tourism_Council" title="World Travel and Tourism Council">World Travel and Tourism Council</a>, attracting over 2.5 million visitors in 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[146]</a></sup> The country is also Asia's number one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[147]</a></sup> The country is popular for the growing practice of <a href="/wiki/Sex_reassignment_surgery" title="Sex reassignment surgery">sex reassignment surgery</a> (SRS) and cosmetic surgery. In 2010–2012, more than 90% of medical tourists travelled to Thailand for SRS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Prostitution_in_Thailand" title="Prostitution in Thailand">Prostitution in Thailand</a> and sex tourism also form a <i>de facto</i> part of the economy. Campaigns promote Thailand as exotic to attract tourists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[149]</a></sup> One estimate published in 2003 placed the trade at US$4.3 billion per year or about 3% of the Thai economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-legal_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-legal-161">[150]</a></sup> It is believed that at least 10% of tourist dollars are spent on the sex trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture_and_natural_resources">Agriculture and natural resources</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Thailand" title="Agriculture in Thailand">Agriculture in Thailand</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1316" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg/300px-Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg/450px-Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg/600px-Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thailand has long been <a href="/wiki/Rice_production_in_Thailand" title="Rice production in Thailand">one of the largest rice exporters in the world</a>. Forty-nine percent of Thailand's labour force is employed in agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-163">[152]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Forty-nine per cent of Thailand's labour force is employed in <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Thailand" title="Agriculture in Thailand">agriculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-163">[152]</a></sup> This is down from 70% in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_163-2"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-163">[152]</a></sup> Rice is the most important crop in the country and Thailand had long been the world's leading exporter of rice, until recently falling behind both India and Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[153]</a></sup> Thailand has the highest percentage of arable land, 27.25%, of any nation in the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Mekong_Subregion" title="Greater Mekong Subregion">Greater Mekong Subregion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[154]</a></sup> About 55% of the arable land area is used for rice production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IRRI_Thailand_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-IRRI_Thailand-166">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>Agriculture has been experiencing a transition from labour-intensive and transitional methods to a more industrialised and competitive sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_163-3"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-163">[152]</a></sup> Between 1962 and 1983, the agricultural sector grew by 4.1% per year on average and continued to grow at 2.2% between 1983 and 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_163-4"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-163">[152]</a></sup> The relative contribution of agriculture to GDP has declined while exports of goods and services have increased.
</p><p>Furthermore, access to <a href="/wiki/Biocapacity" title="Biocapacity">biocapacity</a> in Thailand is lower than world average. In 2016, Thailand had 1.2 global hectares<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GFN_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-GFN-167">[156]</a></sup> of biocapacity per person within its territory, a little less than world average of 1.6 global hectares per person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[157]</a></sup> In contrast, in 2016, they used 2.5 global hectares of biocapacity – their <a href="/wiki/Ecological_footprint" title="Ecological footprint">ecological footprint</a> of consumption. This means they use about twice as much biocapacity as Thailand contains. As a result, Thailand is running a biocapacity deficit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GFN_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-GFN-167">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Informal_economy">Informal economy</span></h3>
<p>Thailand has a diverse and robust informal labour sector—in 2012, it was estimated that informal workers comprised 62.6% of the Thai workforce. The <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Labour_(Thailand)" title="Ministry of Labour (Thailand)">Ministry of Labour</a> defines informal workers to be individuals who work in informal economies and do not have employee status under a given country's Labour Protection Act (LPA). The informal sector in Thailand has grown significantly over the past 60 years over the course of Thailand's gradual transition from an agriculture-based economy to becoming more industrialised and service-oriented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-169">[158]</a></sup> Between 1993 and 1995, ten percent of the Thai labour force moved from the agricultural sector to urban and industrial jobs, especially in the manufacturing sector. It is estimated that between 1988 and 1995, the number of factory workers in the country doubled from two to four million, as Thailand's GDP tripled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-170">[159]</a></sup> While the Asian Financial Crisis that followed in 1997 hit the Thai economy hard, the industrial sector continued to expand under widespread deregulation, as Thailand was mandated to adopt a range of structural adjustment reforms upon receiving funding from the IMF and World Bank. These reforms implemented an agenda of increased privatisation and trade liberalisation in the country, and decreased federal subsidisation of public goods and utilities, agricultural price supports, and regulations on fair wages and labour conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-171">[160]</a></sup> These changes put further pressure on the agricultural sector, and prompted continued migration from the rural countryside to the growing cities. Many migrant farmers found work in Thailand's growing manufacturing industry, and took jobs in sweatshops and factories with few labour regulations and often exploitative conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>Those that could not find formal factory work, including illegal migrants and the families of rural Thai migrants that followed their relatives to the urban centres, turned to the informal sector to provide the extra support needed for survival—under the widespread regulation imposed by the structural adjustment programs, one family member working in a factory or sweatshop made very little. Scholars argue that the economic consequences and social costs of Thailand's labour reforms in the wake of the 1997 Asian Financial Crisis fell on individuals and families rather than the state. This can be described as the "externalisation of market risk", meaning that as the country's labour market became increasingly deregulated, the burden and responsibility of providing an adequate livelihood shifted from employers and the state to the workers themselves, whose families had to find jobs in the informal sector to make up for the losses and subsidise the wages being made by their relatives in the formal sector. The weight of these economic changes hit migrants and the urban poor especially hard, and the informal sector expanded rapidly as a result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-171">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, informal labour in Thailand is typically broken down into three main groups: subcontracted/self employed/home-based workers, service workers (including those that are employed in restaurants, as street vendors, masseuses, taxi drivers, and as domestic workers), and agricultural workers. Not included in these categories are those that work in entertainment, nightlife, and the sex industry. Individuals employed in these facets of the informal labour sector face additional vulnerabilities, including recruitment into circles of sexual exploitation and human trafficking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-169">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>In general, education levels are low in the informal sector. A 2012 study found that 64% of informal workers had not completed education beyond primary school. Many informal workers are also migrants, only some of which have legal status in the country. Education and citizenship are two main barriers to entry for those looking to work in formal industries, and enjoy the labour protections and social security benefits that come along with formal employment. Because the informal labour sector is not recognised under the Labour Protection Act (LPA), informal workers are much more vulnerable labour to exploitation and unsafe working conditions than those employed in more formal and federally recognised industries. While some Thai labour laws provide minimal protections to domestic and agricultural workers, they are often weak and difficult to enforce. Furthermore, Thai social security policies fail to protect against the risks many informal workers face, including workplace accidents and compensation as well as unemployment and retirement insurance. Many informal workers are not legally contracted for their employment, and many do not make a living wage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_169-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-169">[158]</a></sup> Tens of thousands of migrants from neighboring countries face exploitation in a few industries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[162]</a></sup> especially in fishing where slave-like conditions have been reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_technology">Science and technology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scientists_are_working_in_the_lab.14.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="5472" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Scientists_are_working_in_the_lab.14.jpg/220px-Scientists_are_working_in_the_lab.14.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Scientists_are_working_in_the_lab.14.jpg/330px-Scientists_are_working_in_the_lab.14.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Scientists_are_working_in_the_lab.14.jpg/440px-Scientists_are_working_in_the_lab.14.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scientists_are_working_in_the_lab.14.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scientists are working in the lab</div></div></div>
<p>Numerous Thai scientists have made significant contributions in various fields of science and technology. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Krisana_Kraisintu" title="Krisana Kraisintu">Krisana Kraisintu</a>, who is known as the "Gypsy pharmacist" for her development of the first generic HIV "cocktail" drug known as GPO-VIR.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SDN-20191030_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-SDN-20191030-175">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[165]</a></sup> Her efforts have significantly reduced treatment costs, and GPO-VIR was endorsed by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> as the first regimen treatment for <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a> patients in poor countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[166]</a></sup> In Thailand, GPO-VIR is used in the national HIV/AIDS treatment programme, making it free of charge for 100,000 patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[167]</a></sup> Another example is Pongrama Ramasoota, who developed the production of therapeutic human monoclonal antibodies against <a href="/wiki/Dengue_virus" title="Dengue virus">dengue virus</a> and the world's first <a href="/wiki/Dengue_fever" title="Dengue fever">Dengue fever</a> medication, which also includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DNA_vaccination" title="DNA vaccination">DNA vaccine</a> development for dengue and <a href="/wiki/Canine_parvovirus" title="Canine parvovirus">Canine parvovirus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thailand has also made significant advances technology in the development of Medical <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">Robotics</a>. Medical robots have been used and promoted in Thailand in many areas, including <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">surgery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diagnosis" title="Diagnosis">diagnosis</a>, rehabilitation and services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[169]</a></sup> In surgery, in 2021, <a href="/wiki/Mahidol_University" title="Mahidol University">Mahidol University</a>'s "BART LAB" team successfully researched the invention of the robot-assisted surgery (<a href="/wiki/Minimally_invasive_procedure" title="Minimally invasive procedure">Minimal Invasive Surgery</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[170]</a></sup> back in 2019, The Medical Services Department has unveiled Thailand's robot created to help surgeons in brain surgery on patients afflicted with epilepsy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[171]</a></sup> back in 2017, <a href="/wiki/Ramathibodi_Hospital" title="Ramathibodi Hospital">Ramathibodi Hospital</a>, a leading government hospital in Bangkok and a reputable medical school, successfully performed the first robot-assisted brain surgery in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[172]</a></sup> For rehabilitation and therapy robots, were developed to help patients with arm and leg injuries perform practiced movements aided by the robots is the first prize winner of the i-MEDBOT Innovation Contest 2018 held by <a href="/wiki/Thailand_Center_of_Excellence_for_Life_Sciences" title="Thailand Center of Excellence for Life Sciences">Thailand Center of Excellence for Life Sciences</a> (TCELS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_Institute_for_Statistics" title="UNESCO Institute for Statistics">UNESCO Institute for Statistics</a>, Thailand devoted 1% of its GDP to science research and development in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[174]</a></sup> Between 2014 and 2016, Research and development workforce in Thailand increased from 84,216 people to 112,386 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[175]</a></sup> Thailand was ranked 43rd in the <a href="/wiki/Global_Innovation_Index" title="Global Innovation Index">Global Innovation Index</a> in 2021<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[179]</a></sup> The Thai government is developing new
growth hubs by starting with the
<a href="/wiki/Eastern_Economic_Corridor" title="Eastern Economic Corridor">Eastern Economic Corridor</a> of Innovation (EECi) to accelerating human resource and research development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[180]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Science_and_Technology_Development_Agency" title="National Science and Technology Development Agency">National Science and Technology Development Agency</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Government_agency" title="Government agency">agency</a> of the government of Thailand which supports research in science and technology and its application in the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Thailand" title="Economy of Thailand">Thai economy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>By December 2020 with 308.35 Mbit/s Thailand had become world leader in terms of Internet fixed broadband internet speed, with Switzerland and France in Europe in positions 5 and 8 respectively, with the US at position 10 with 173.67 Mbit/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BKKPost_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-BKKPost-193">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">Infrastructure</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Thailand" title="Transport in Thailand">Transport in Thailand</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_airports_in_Thailand" title="List of airports in Thailand">List of airports in Thailand</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2322" data-file-width="3497" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg/200px-Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg/300px-Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg/400px-Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/BTS_Skytrain" title="BTS Skytrain">BTS Skytrain</a> is an elevated rapid transit system in Bangkok</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/State_Railway_of_Thailand" title="State Railway of Thailand">State Railway of Thailand</a> (SRT) operates all of Thailand's national rail lines. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bangkok_Railway_Station" title="Bangkok Railway Station">Bangkok Railway Station</a> (Hua Lamphong Station) is the main terminus of all routes. Phahonyothin and ICD <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lat_Krabang" title="Lat Krabang">Lat Krabang</a> are the main freight terminals. As of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Thailand&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> SRT had 4,507 km (2,801 mi) of track, all of it <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meter_gauge" title="Meter gauge">meter gauge</a> except the Airport Link. Nearly all is single-track (4,097 km), although some important sections around Bangkok are double (303 km or 188 mi) or triple-tracked (107 km or 66 mi) and there are plans to extend this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[183]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport_in_Bangkok" title="Rail transport in Bangkok">Rail transport in Bangkok</a> includes long-distance services, and some daily commuter trains running from and to the outskirts of the city during the rush hour, but passenger numbers have remained low. There are also three rapid transit rail systems in the capital.
</p><p>Thailand has 390,000 kilometres (240,000 miles) of highways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAR-20170123_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAR-20170123-195">[184]</a></sup> As of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Thailand&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Thailand has over 462,133 roads and 37 million registered vehicles, 20 million of them motorbikes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[185]</a></sup> A number of undivided two-lane highways have been converted into divided four-lane highways. There are 4,125 public vans operating on 114 routes from Bangkok alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[186]</a></sup> Other forms of road transport includes <a href="/wiki/Auto_rickshaw" title="Auto rickshaw">tuk-tuks</a>, taxis—with over 80,647 registered taxis nationwide as of 2018<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[187]</a></sup>—vans (<a href="/wiki/Minibus" title="Minibus">minibus</a>), motorbike taxis and <a href="/wiki/Songthaew" title="Songthaew">songthaews</a>.
</p><p>As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Thailand&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Thailand has 103 airports with 63 paved runways, in addition to 6 heliports. The busiest airport in the county is Bangkok's <a href="/wiki/Suvarnabhumi_Airport" title="Suvarnabhumi Airport">Suvarnabhumi Airport</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Thailand" title="Energy in Thailand">Energy in Thailand</a></div>
<p>75% of Thailand's electrical generation is powered by <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Energy_Security_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-Energy_Security-199">[188]</a></sup> Coal-fired power plants produce an additional 20% of electricity, with the remainder coming from biomass, hydro, and biogas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Energy_Security_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-Energy_Security-199">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thailand produces roughly one-third of the oil it consumes. It is the second largest importer of oil in SE Asia. Thailand is a large producer of natural gas, with reserves of at least 10 trillion cubic feet. After Indonesia, it is the largest coal producer in SE Asia, but must import additional coal to meet domestic demand.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Thailand" title="Demographics of Thailand">Demographics of Thailand</a></div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="610" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png/220px-Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png/330px-Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png/440px-Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Population pyramid 2016</div></div></div>
<p>Thailand had a population of 69,799,978 as of 2020.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Thailand's population is largely rural, concentrated in the rice-growing areas of the central, northeastern and northern regions. About 45.7% of Thailand's population lived in urban areas as of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Thailand&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, concentrated mostly in and around the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bangkok_Metropolitan_Area" title="Bangkok Metropolitan Area">Bangkok Metropolitan Area</a>.
</p><p>Thailand's government-sponsored family planning program resulted in a dramatic decline in population growth from 3.1% in 1960 to around 0.4% today. In 1970, an average of 5.7 people lived in a Thai household. At the time of the 2010 census, the average Thai household size was 3.2 people.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Thailand" title="Ethnic groups in Thailand">Ethnic groups in Thailand</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thailand_(272021437).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1080" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Thailand_%28272021437%29.jpg/220px-Thailand_%28272021437%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Thailand_%28272021437%29.jpg/330px-Thailand_%28272021437%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Thailand_%28272021437%29.jpg/440px-Thailand_%28272021437%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thailand_(272021437).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hill tribes girls in the Northeast of Thailand</div></div></div>
<p>Thai nationals make up the majority of Thailand's population, 95.9% in 2010. The remaining 4.1% of the population are Burmese (2.0%), others 1.3%, and unspecified 0.9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the Royal Thai Government's 2011 Country Report to the UN Committee responsible for the <a href="/wiki/International_Convention_on_the_Elimination_of_All_Forms_of_Racial_Discrimination" title="International Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination">International Convention for the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination</a>, available from the Department of Rights and Liberties Promotion of the Thai Ministry of Justice,<sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 3">: 3 </span></sup> 62 ethnic communities are officially recognised in Thailand. Twenty million Central Thai (together with approximately 650,000 <a href="/wiki/Khorat_Thai" title="Khorat Thai">Khorat Thai</a>) make up approximately 20,650,000 (34.1 percent) of the nation's population of 60,544,937<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[189]</a></sup> at the time of completion of the Mahidol University <i>Ethnolinguistic Maps of Thailand</i> data (1997).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMT_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-EMT-201">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 2011 Thailand Country Report provides population numbers for mountain peoples ('hill tribes') and ethnic communities in the Northeast and is explicit about its main reliance on the Mahidol University Ethnolinguistic Maps of Thailand data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMT_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-EMT-201">[190]</a></sup> Thus, though over 3.288 million people in the Northeast alone could not be categorised, the population and percentages of other ethnic communities circa 1997 are known for all of Thailand and constitute minimum populations. In descending order, the largest (equal to or greater than 400,000) are a) 15,080,000 Lao (24.9 percent) consisting of the Thai Lao (14 million) and other smaller Lao groups, namely the Thai Loei (400–500,000), Lao Lom (350,000), Lao Wiang/Klang (200,000), Lao Khrang (90,000), Lao Ngaew (30,000), and Lao Ti (10,000; b) six million Khon Muang (9.9 percent, also called Northern Thais); c) 4.5 million Pak Tai (7.5 percent, also called Southern Thais); d) 1.4 million Khmer Leu (2.3 percent, also called Northern Khmer); e) 900,000 Malay (1.5%); f) 500,000 Nyaw (0.8 percent); g) 470,000 Phu Thai (0.8 percent); h) 400,000 Kuy/Kuay (also known as Suay) (0.7 percent), and i) 350,000 Karen (0.6 percent).<sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7–13">: 7–13 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thai_Chinese" title="Thai Chinese">Thai Chinese</a>, those of significant Chinese heritage, are 14% of the population, while Thais with partial Chinese ancestry comprise up to 40% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theraphan_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-Theraphan-202">[191]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thai_Malays" title="Thai Malays">Thai Malays</a> represent 3% of the population, with the remainder consisting of <a href="/wiki/Mon_people" title="Mon people">Mons</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khmers" title="Khmers">Khmers</a> and various "<a href="/wiki/Hill_tribe_(Thailand)" title="Hill tribe (Thailand)">hill tribes</a>". The country's official language is <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a> and the primary religion is <a href="/wiki/Theravada" title="Theravada">Theravada</a> Buddhism, which is practised by around 95% of the population.
</p><p>Increasing numbers of migrants from neighbouring Myanmar, Laos, and Cambodia, as well as from Nepal and India, have pushed the total number of non-national residents to around 3.5 million as of 2009<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Thailand&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, up from an estimated 2 million in 2008, and about 1.3 million in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[192]</a></sup> Some 41,000 <a href="/wiki/British_people" title="British people">Britons</a> and 20,000 <a href="/wiki/Australians" title="Australians">Australians</a> live in Thailand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FM_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FM-205">[194]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_centres">Population centres</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Thailand" title="List of cities in Thailand">List of cities in Thailand</a></div>
<div>

<td rowspan="11" style="text-align: center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:View_from_Baiyoke_Sky_Hotel,_Bangkok_(7053110333)_cropped.jpg" title="Bangkok"><img alt="Bangkok" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="958" data-file-width="1916" decoding="async" height="60" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/View_from_Baiyoke_Sky_Hotel%2C_Bangkok_%287053110333%29_cropped.jpg/120px-View_from_Baiyoke_Sky_Hotel%2C_Bangkok_%287053110333%29_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/View_from_Baiyoke_Sky_Hotel%2C_Bangkok_%287053110333%29_cropped.jpg/180px-View_from_Baiyoke_Sky_Hotel%2C_Bangkok_%287053110333%29_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/View_from_Baiyoke_Sky_Hotel%2C_Bangkok_%287053110333%29_cropped.jpg/240px-View_from_Baiyoke_Sky_Hotel%2C_Bangkok_%287053110333%29_cropped.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a><br/><a href="/wiki/Bangkok_Metropolitan_Administration" title="Bangkok Metropolitan Administration">Bangkok</a><br/><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nonthaburi_-_Bangbuathong.jpg" title="Nonthaburi City"><img alt="Nonthaburi City" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="797" data-file-width="1417" decoding="async" height="67" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Nonthaburi_-_Bangbuathong.jpg/120px-Nonthaburi_-_Bangbuathong.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Nonthaburi_-_Bangbuathong.jpg/180px-Nonthaburi_-_Bangbuathong.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Nonthaburi_-_Bangbuathong.jpg/240px-Nonthaburi_-_Bangbuathong.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a><br/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonthaburi_(city)" title="Nonthaburi (city)">Nonthaburi City</a>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Thailand" title="Languages of Thailand">Languages of Thailand</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:124px;max-width:124px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:215px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thailand_ethnic_map.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1849" data-file-width="1051" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Thailand_ethnic_map.svg/122px-Thailand_ethnic_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Thailand_ethnic_map.svg/183px-Thailand_ethnic_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Thailand_ethnic_map.svg/244px-Thailand_ethnic_map.svg.png 2x" width="122"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">An ethnolinguistic map of Thailand.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:164px;max-width:164px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:215px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bangkok_National_Museum_-_2017-04-22_(008).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Bangkok_National_Museum_-_2017-04-22_%28008%29.jpg/162px-Bangkok_National_Museum_-_2017-04-22_%28008%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Bangkok_National_Museum_-_2017-04-22_%28008%29.jpg/243px-Bangkok_National_Museum_-_2017-04-22_%28008%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Bangkok_National_Museum_-_2017-04-22_%28008%29.jpg/324px-Bangkok_National_Museum_-_2017-04-22_%28008%29.jpg 2x" width="162"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">The Silajaruek of <a href="/wiki/Sukhothai_Kingdom" title="Sukhothai Kingdom">Sukhothai Kingdom</a> are hundreds of stone inscriptions that form a historical record of the period.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The official language of Thailand is <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kra%E2%80%93Dai_language" title="Kra–Dai language">Kra–Dai language</a> closely related to <a href="/wiki/Lao_language" title="Lao language">Lao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shan_language" title="Shan language">Shan</a> in Myanmar, and numerous smaller languages spoken in an arc from <a href="/wiki/Hainan" title="Hainan">Hainan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a> south to the Chinese border. It is the principal language of education and government and spoken throughout the country. The standard is based on the dialect of the central Thai people, and it is written in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thai_alphabet" title="Thai alphabet">Thai alphabet</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Abugida" title="Abugida">abugida</a> script that evolved from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khmer_alphabet" title="Khmer alphabet">Khmer alphabet</a>.
</p><p>Sixty-two languages were recognised by the Royal Thai Government. For the purposes of the national census, four dialects of Thai exist; these partly coincide with regional designations, such as <a href="/wiki/Southern_Thai_language" title="Southern Thai language">Southern Thai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Thai_language" title="Northern Thai language">Northern Thai</a>.
</p><p>The largest of Thailand's minority languages is the <a href="/wiki/Lao_language" title="Lao language">Lao</a> dialect of <a href="/wiki/Isan_language" title="Isan language">Isan</a> spoken in the northeastern provinces. Although sometimes considered a Thai dialect, it is a Lao dialect, and the region where it is traditionally spoken was historically part of the Lao kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Lan_Xang" title="Lan Xang">Lan Xang</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In the far south, <a href="/wiki/Kelantan-Pattani_Malay" title="Kelantan-Pattani Malay">Kelantan-Pattani Malay</a> is the primary language of Malay Muslims. Varieties of Chinese are also spoken by the large <a href="/wiki/Thai_Chinese" title="Thai Chinese">Thai Chinese</a> population, with the <a href="/wiki/Teochew_dialect" title="Teochew dialect">Teochew dialect</a> best-represented.
</p><p>Numerous tribal languages are also spoken, including many <a href="/wiki/Austroasiatic_languages" title="Austroasiatic languages">Austroasiatic languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Mon_language" title="Mon language">Mon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khmer_language" title="Khmer language">Khmer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Viet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mlabri_language" title="Mlabri language">Mlabri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aslian_languages" title="Aslian languages">Aslian</a>; <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Cham_language" title="Cham language">Cham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moken_language" title="Moken language">Moken</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urak_Lawoi%27_language" title="Urak Lawoi' language">Urak Lawoi'</a>; <a href="/wiki/Sino-Tibetan_languages" title="Sino-Tibetan languages">Sino-Tibetan languages</a> like <a href="/wiki/Lawa_language" title="Lawa language">Lawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Akha_language" title="Akha language">Akha</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karen_languages" title="Karen languages">Karen</a>; and other <a href="/wiki/Tai_languages" title="Tai languages">Tai languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Tai_Yo_language" title="Tai Yo language">Tai Yo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phu_Thai_language" title="Phu Thai language">Phu Thai</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Saek_language" title="Saek language">Saek</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hmong_language" title="Hmong language">Hmong</a> is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Hmong%E2%80%93Mien_languages" title="Hmong–Mien languages">Hmong–Mien languages</a>, which is now regarded as a language family of its own.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Thailand" title="Religion in Thailand">Religion in Thailand</a></div>
<div class="barbox tleft" style="overflow-x: auto;">
<div style="border:1px solid silver; font-size:88%; padding:0.4em; width:auto; background: white;">
<table style="text-align:left; border-collapse:collapse; width:100%;">

<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 1.2em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">0.003%</td>
